      <p>
       <b>
        Sardinian
       </b>
       (
       <i>
        sardu
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        limba sarda
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        lingua sarda
       </i>
       ) or
       <b>
        Sard
       </b>
       is the primary
       <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_language" title="Indigenous language">
        indigenous
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance language
       </a>
       spoken on most of the island of
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">
        Sardinia
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       ). Among the Romance languages, it is considered one of the closest genealogical descendants, if not the closest, to
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, it also incorporates a Pre-Latin (mostly
       <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Sardinian_language" title="Paleo-Sardinian language">
        Paleo-Sardinian
       </a>
       , also known as
       <a href="/wiki/Nuragic_civilization" title="Nuragic civilization">
        Nuragic
       </a>
       , and, to a much lesser degree,
       <a href="/wiki/Punic_language" title="Punic language">
        Punic
       </a>
       )
       <a href="/wiki/Stratum_(linguistics)#substratum" title="Stratum (linguistics)">
        substratum
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greek" title="Byzantine Greek">
        Byzantine Greek
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superstratum" title="Superstratum">
        superstratum
       </a>
       due to the past political membership of the island, first falling into the Iberian sphere of influence and later towards the Italian one.
      </p>
      <p>
       Sardinian is traditionally thought to consist of two mutually intelligible
       <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">
        varieties
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sardinianvarieties_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sardinianvarieties-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Campidanese_dialect" title="Campidanese dialect">
        Campidanese
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Logudorese_dialect" title="Logudorese dialect">
        Logudorese
       </a>
       , spoken respectively in the Southern half and in the North-Central part of Sardinia; the view of there being a definite dialectal boundary has been subjected to more recent research, that shows a continuum between the Northern and Southern areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some attempts have been made to introduce a standardized writing system for administrative purposes, like the LSU (
       <i>
        Limba Sarda Unificada
       </i>
       , "Unified Sardinian Language") and then the LSC (
       <i>
        Limba Sarda Comuna
       </i>
       , "Common Sardinian Language"),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but they have not been generally acknowledged by native speakers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Britannica-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1997, Sardinian was recognized by a regional law, along with other languages spoken on the island; since 1999, Sardinian is also one of the twelve "historical
       <a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">
        language minorities
       </a>
       " of Italy and protected as such by the national Law no. 482/1999.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the vitality of the Sardinian-speaking community is threatened and
       <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">
        UNESCO
       </a>
       classifies the language as "definitely
       <a href="/wiki/Endangered_language" title="Endangered language">
        endangered
       </a>
       ";
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although an estimated 68.4 percent of the islanders have a good oral command of Sardinian,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Italian is displacing it nonetheless in many cases, and the language ability, while being prevalent among the older generation beyond retirement age, has been estimated to have dropped in fact to around 13 percent among children.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maxia_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Maxia-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Euromosaic_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Euromosaic-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">
        Overview
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Now the question arises as to whether Sardinian is to be considered a dialect or a language in its own right. Politically speaking,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-specification_18-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-specification-18">
          [18]
         </a>
        </sup>
        it is clearly one of the many dialects
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-specification_18-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-specification-18">
          [18]
         </a>
        </sup>
        of Italy, just like the
        <a href="/wiki/Serbo-Croatian" title="Serbo-Croatian">
         Serbo-Croatian
        </a>
        and the
        <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">
         Albanian
        </a>
        spoken in various villages of
        <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">
         Calabria
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">
         Sicily
        </a>
        . However, from a linguistic point of view, that is a different question. It can be said that Sardinian has no relationship whatsoever with any dialect of mainland Italy; it is an archaic Romance speech with its own distinctive characteristics, showing a very original vocabulary in addition to morphology and syntax rather different from the Italian dialects.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
         <a href="#cite_note-19">
          [19]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         <a href="/wiki/Max_Leopold_Wagner" title="Max Leopold Wagner">
          Max Leopold Wagner
         </a>
         ,
         <i>
          La lingua sarda
         </i>
         , 1951 – Ilisso, pp. 90–91
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Sardinian is considered the most
       <a href="/wiki/Conservative_(language)" title="Conservative (language)">
        conservative
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance language
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and its
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substratum" title="Substratum">
        substratum
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Sardinian_language" title="Paleo-Sardinian language">
        Paleo-Sardinian
       </a>
       or Nuragic) has also been researched. A 1949 study by Italian-American linguist
       <a href="/wiki/Mario_Pei" title="Mario Pei">
        Mario Pei
       </a>
       , analyzing the degree of difference from a language's parent (
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       , in the case of
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       ) by comparing
       <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">
        phonology
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">
        inflection
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">
        syntax
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">
        vocabulary
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Intonation_(linguistics)" title="Intonation (linguistics)">
        intonation
       </a>
       , indicated the following percentages (the higher the percentage, the greater the distance from Latin):
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sardinian 8%,
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       12%,
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       20%,
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">
        Romanian
       </a>
       23.5%,
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">
        Occitan
       </a>
       25%,
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       31%, and
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       44%. For example, Latin "
       <i>
        Pone mihi tres panes in bertula
       </i>
       " (put three loaves of bread [from home] in the bag for me) would be the very similar "
       <i>
        Ponemi tres panes in bertula
       </i>
       " in Sardinian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Romance-lg-classification-en.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="556" data-file-width="1011" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Romance-lg-classification-en.svg/450px-Romance-lg-classification-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Romance-lg-classification-en.svg/675px-Romance-lg-classification-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Romance-lg-classification-en.svg/900px-Romance-lg-classification-en.svg.png 2x" width="450"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Romance-lg-classification-en.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Chart of Romance languages based on structural and comparative criteria (not on socio-functional ones). Koryakov (2001) ascribes Sardinian to the separated Island Romance branch of the Romance languages, along with old Corsican (
         <a href="/wiki/Corsican_language" title="Corsican language">
          modern Corsican
         </a>
         is in fact part of the broad Italo-Romance family).
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koryakov_23-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Koryakov-23">
           [23]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Compared to the mainland Italian dialects, Sardinian is virtually incomprehensible for
       <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">
        Italians
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_12-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Britannica-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       being actually an autonomous linguistic group.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sardinia" title="History of Sardinia">
        History of Sardinia
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Sardinia's relative isolation from mainland Europe encouraged the development of a Romance language preserving traces of its indigenous, pre-Roman language(s). The language is posited to have substratal influences from
       <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Sardinian_language" title="Paleo-Sardinian language">
        Paleo-Sardinian language
       </a>
       , which some scholars have linked to
       <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">
        Basque
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_language" title="Etruscan language">
        Etruscan
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pittau.it_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pittau.it-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Stratum_(linguistics)#Adstratum" title="Stratum (linguistics)">
        Adstratal
       </a>
       influences include
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       . The situation of Sardinian language with regard to the politically dominant ones did not change until the 1950s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosita_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rosita-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ministero_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ministero-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Origins.2C_Prenuragic_and_Nuragic_era">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origins,_Prenuragic_and_Nuragic_era">
        Origins, Prenuragic and Nuragic era
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origins, Prenuragic and Nuragic era">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Sardinian_language" title="Paleo-Sardinian language">
        Paleo-Sardinian language
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The origins of the Paleo-Sardinian language are currently not known. Research has attempted to discover obscure, indigenous, pre-Romance
       <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">
        roots
       </a>
       ; the root
       <i>
        s(a)rd
       </i>
       , present in many place names and denoting the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sardinians" title="Sardinians">
        island's people
       </a>
       , is reportedly from the
       <a href="/wiki/Sherden" title="Sherden">
        Sherden
       </a>
       (one of the
       <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">
        Sea Peoples
       </a>
       ), although this assertion is hotly debated.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1984, Massimo Pittau said he found in the
       <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_language" title="Etruscan language">
        Etruscan language
       </a>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">
        etymology
       </a>
       of many Latin words after comparing it with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuragic_language" title="Nuragic language">
        Nuragic
       </a>
       language(s).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pittau.it_27-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-pittau.it-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Etruscan elements, formerly thought to have originated in Latin, would indicate a connection between the ancient Sardinian culture and the Etruscans. According to Pittau, the Etruscan and Nuragic language(s) are descended from
       <a href="/wiki/Lydian_language" title="Lydian language">
        Lydian
       </a>
       (and therefore
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
        Indo-European
       </a>
       ) as a consequence of contact with Etruscans and other
       <a href="/wiki/Tyrrhenians" title="Tyrrhenians">
        Tyrrhenians
       </a>
       from
       <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">
        Sardis
       </a>
       as described by
       <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">
        Herodotus
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pittau.it_27-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-pittau.it-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although Pittau suggests that the Tirrenii landed in Sardinia and the Etruscans landed in modern
       <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">
        Tuscany
       </a>
       , his views are not shared by most Etruscologists.
      </p>
      <p>
       According to Alberto Areddu
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Sherden" title="Sherden">
        Sherden
       </a>
       were of Illyrian origin, on the basis of some lexical elements, unanimously acknowledged as belonging to the indigenous Substrate. Areddu asserts that in ancient Sardinia, especially in the most interior area (Barbagia and Ogliastra), the locals supposedly spoke a particular branch of Indo-European. There are in fact some correspondences, both formal and semantic, with the few testimonies of Illyrian (or Thracian) languages, and above all with their claimed linguistical continuer,
       <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">
        Albanian
       </a>
       . He finds such correlations: Sard.
       <i>
        eni, enis, eniu
       </i>
       'yew' = Alb.
       <i>
        enjë
       </i>
       'yew'; Sard.
       <i>
        urtzula
       </i>
       'clematis' = Alb.
       <i>
        urth
       </i>
       'ivy'; Sard.
       <i>
        rethi
       </i>
       'tendril' = Alb.
       <i>
        rrypthi
       </i>
       'tendril'.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Recently he also discovered important correlations with the Balkan bird world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       According to Bertoldi and Terracini, Paleo-Sardinian has similarities with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberic_language" title="Iberic language">
        Iberic languages
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siculi" title="Siculi">
        Siculian
       </a>
       ; for example, the suffix
       <i>
        -ara
       </i>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Proparoxytone" title="Proparoxytone">
        proparoxytones
       </a>
       indicated the plural. Terracini proposed the same for suffixes in -
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /àna/
       </span>
       , -/ànna/, -/énna/, -/ònna/ +
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /r/
       </span>
       + a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paragogic" title="Paragogic">
        paragogic
       </a>
       vowel (such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Toponymy" title="Toponymy">
        toponym
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Bonnanaro" title="Bonnanaro">
         Bunnànnaru
        </a>
       </i>
       ). Rohlfs, Butler and Craddock add the suffix -
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ini/
       </span>
       (such as the toponym
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Barumini" title="Barumini">
         Barùmini
        </a>
       </i>
       ) as a unique element of Paleo-Sardinian. Suffixes in /a, e, o, u/ + -rr- found a correspondence in
       <a href="/wiki/Berber_languages" title="Berber languages">
        north Africa
       </a>
       (Terracini), in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">
        Iberia
       </a>
       (Blasco Ferrer) and in
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">
        southern Italy
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Gascony" title="Gascony">
        Gascony
       </a>
       (Rohlfs), with a closer relationship to Basque (Wagner and Hubschmid). However, these early links to a
       <a href="/wiki/Aquitanian_language" title="Aquitanian language">
        Basque precursor
       </a>
       have been questioned by some Basque linguists.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trask_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Trask-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to Terracini, suffixes in -
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ài/
       </span>
       , -
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /éi/
       </span>
       , -
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /òi/
       </span>
       , and -
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ùi/
       </span>
       are common to Paleo-Sardinian and northern African languages. Pittau emphasized that this concerns terms originally ending in an accented vowel, with an attached paragogic vowel; the suffix resisted Latinization in some place names, which show a Latin body and a Nuragic
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/desinence" title="wikt:desinence">
        suffix
       </a>
       . According to Bertoldi, some toponyms ending in -
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ài/
       </span>
       and -/asài/ indicated an Anatolic influence. The suffix -/aiko/, widely used in Iberia and possibly of Celtic origin, and the ethnic suffix in -/itanos/ and -/etanos/ (for example, the Sardinian
       <i>
        Sulcitanos
       </i>
       ) have also been noted as Paleo-Sardinian elements (Terracini, Ribezzo, Wagner, Hubschmid and Faust).
      </p>
      <p>
       Linguists Blasco Ferrer (2009, 2010) and Morvan (2009) have attempted to revive a theoretical connection with Basque by linking words such as Sardinian
       <i>
        ospile
       </i>
       "fresh grazing for cattle" and Basque
       <i>
        ozpil
       </i>
       ; Sardinian
       <i>
        arrotzeri
       </i>
       "vagabond" and Basque
       <i>
        arrotz
       </i>
       "stranger"; Gallurese (South Corsican and North Sardinian)
       <i>
        zerru
       </i>
       "pig" and Basque
       <i>
        zerri
       </i>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_people#Genetic_peculiarities_of_the_population" title="Sardinian people">
        Genetic data
       </a>
       on the distribution of HLA antigens have suggested a common origin for the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basque_people" title="Basque people">
        Basques
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_people" title="Sardinian people">
        Sardinians
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnaiz-Villena_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Arnaiz-Villena-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Since the Neolithic period, some degree of variance across the island's regions is also attested. The
       <a href="/wiki/Arzachena_culture" title="Arzachena culture">
        Arzachena culture
       </a>
       , for instance, suggests a link between the northernmost Sardinian region (
       <a href="/wiki/Gallura" title="Gallura">
        Gallura
       </a>
       ) and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Corsica" title="Southern Corsica">
        southern Corsica
       </a>
       that finds further confirmation in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naturalis_Historia" title="Naturalis Historia">
        Naturalis Historia
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">
        Pliny the Elder
       </a>
       . There are also some stylistic differences across Northern and Southern Nuragic Sardinia, which may indicate the existence of two other tribal groups (
       <a href="/wiki/Balares" title="Balares">
        Balares
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ilienses" title="Ilienses">
        Ilienses
       </a>
       ) mentioned by the same Roman author. According to the archeologist Giovanni Ugas,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       these tribes may have in fact played a role in shaping the current regional linguistic differences of the island.
      </p>
      <p>
       <span id="Other_influences">
       </span>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_period">
        Roman period
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Roman period">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Although
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sardinia#Roman_Empire" title="History of Sardinia">
        Roman
       </a>
       domination, which began in 238
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        b.c.
       </span>
       , brought
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       to Sardinia, it was unable to completely supplant the pre-Latin Sardinian languages, including
       <a href="/wiki/Punic_language" title="Punic language">
        Punic
       </a>
       , which continued to be spoken in the
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        a.d.
       </span>
       4th century as attested by votive inscriptions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some obscure Nuragic roots remained unchanged, and in many cases the Latin accepted local roots (like
       <i>
        nur
       </i>
       , which makes its appearance in
       <a href="/wiki/Nuraghe" title="Nuraghe">
        nuraghe
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nurra" title="Nurra">
         Nurra
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nurri" title="Nurri">
         Nurri
        </a>
       </i>
       and many other toponyms).
       <a href="/wiki/Barbagia" title="Barbagia">
        Barbagia
       </a>
       , the mountainous central region of the island, derives its name from the Latin
       <i>
        Barbaria
       </i>
       , because its people refused cultural and linguistic assimilation for a long time: 50% of toponyms of central Sardinia, particulary in the territory of
       <a href="/wiki/Olzai" title="Olzai">
        Olzai
       </a>
       , are actually not related to any known language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Besides the place names, on the island there are still a few names of plants, animals and geological formations directly traceable to the ancient Nuragic era.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">
        Cicero
       </a>
       called the Sardinian rebels
       <i>
        latrones mastrucati
       </i>
       ("thieves with rough wool cloaks") to emphasize Roman superiority.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the long Roman domination Latin gradually become however the speech of the majority of the island's inhabitants.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a result of this process of
       <a href="/wiki/Romanization_(cultural)" title="Romanization (cultural)">
        romanization
       </a>
       the Sardinian language is today classified as Romance or neo-Latin, with some phonetic features resembling
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">
        Old Latin
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Classification_of_Romance_languages#The_standard_proposal" title="Classification of Romance languages">
        Some linguists
       </a>
       assert that modern Sardinian, being part of the Island Romance group,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koryakov_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Koryakov-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       was the first language to split off from Latin, all others evolving from Latin as Continental Romance.
      </p>
      <p>
       At that time, the only literature being produced in Sardinia was mostly in Latin: the native (Paleo-Sardinian) and non-native (Punic) pre-Roman languages were then already extinct (the last Punic inscription in Bithia, southern Sardinia, is from the second or third century A.D.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ). Some engraved poems in ancient Greek and Latin (the two most prestigious languages in the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
        Roman Empire
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) are to be seen in Viper Cave, Cagliari, (
       <i>
        Grutta 'e sa Pibera
       </i>
       in Sardinian,
       <i>
        Grotta della Vipera
       </i>
       in Italian,
       <i>
        Cripta Serpentum
       </i>
       in Latin), a burial monument built by Lucius Cassius Philippus (a Roman who had been exiled to Sardinia) in remembrance of his dead spouse Atilia Pomptilla. We also have some religious works by
       <a href="/wiki/Lucifer_of_Cagliari" title="Lucifer of Cagliari">
        Saint Lucifer
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Vercelli" title="Eusebius of Vercelli">
        Eusebius
       </a>
       , both from Caralis (
       <a href="/wiki/Cagliari" title="Cagliari">
        Cagliari
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       Although Sardinia was culturally influenced and politically ruled by the
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
        Byzantine Empire
       </a>
       for almost five centuries, Greek did not enter the language except for some ritual or formal expressions in Sardinian using Greek structure and, sometimes, the Greek alphabet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Evidence for this is found in the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Condaghe" title="Condaghe">
         condaghes
        </a>
       </i>
       , the first written documents in Sardinian. From the long Byzantine era there are only a few entries but they already provide a glimpse of the sociolinguistical situation on the island in which, in addition to the community's everyday Neo-Latin language, Greek was also spoken by the ruling classes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some toponyms, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Jerzu" title="Jerzu">
        Jerzu
       </a>
       (thought to derive from the Greek
       <i>
        khérsos
       </i>
       , "untilled"), together with the personal names Mikhaleis, Konstantine and Basilis, demonstrate Greek influence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_45-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">
        Muslims
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam_in_southern_Italy" title="History of Islam in southern Italy">
        conquered southern Italy and Sicily
       </a>
       , communications broke down between
       <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">
        Constantinople
       </a>
       and Sardinia, whose districts became progressively more autonomous from the Byzantine
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oecumene" title="Oecumene">
        oecumene
       </a>
       (Greek: οἰκουμένη). Sardinia was then brought back into the Latin cultural sphere.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Judgedoms_period">
        <span id="[[Giudicati|Giudicati_(Judicados)_period]]">
        </span>
        Judgedoms period
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Judgedoms period">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Giudicati" title="Giudicati">
        Giudicati
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Sardinian was the first Romance language to gain official status, being used by the four
       <a href="/wiki/Giudicati" title="Giudicati">
        Giudicati
       </a>
       ("Judgedoms"),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       former quarrelling Byzantine districts that became independent political entities after the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">
        Arab expansion
       </a>
       in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean_sea" title="Mediterranean sea">
        Mediterranean
       </a>
       cut off any ties left between the island and
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">
        Byzantium
       </a>
       . Old Sardinian had a greater number of
       <a href="/wiki/Archaism" title="Archaism">
        archaisms
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Latinism" title="Latinism">
        Latinisms
       </a>
       than the present language does. While the earlier documents show the existence of an early Sardinian Koine,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the language used by the various Judgedoms already displayed a certain range of dialectal variation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ministero_29-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ministero-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A special position was occupied by the
       <a href="/wiki/Giudicato_of_Arborea" title="Giudicato of Arborea">
        Giudicato of Arborea
       </a>
       , the last Sardinian kingdom to fall to foreign powers, in which a transitional dialect was spoken, that of Middle Sardinian. The
       <a href="/wiki/Carta_de_Logu" title="Carta de Logu">
        Carta de Logu
       </a>
       of the Kingdom of Arborea, one of the first constitutions in history drawn up in 1355–1376 by
       <a href="/wiki/Marianus_IV_of_Arborea" title="Marianus IV of Arborea">
        Marianus IV
       </a>
       and the Queen, the "Lady Judge" (
       <i>
        judikessa
       </i>
       in Sardinian,
       <i>
        jutgessa
       </i>
       in Catalan,
       <i>
        giudicessa
       </i>
       in Italian)
       <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Arborea" title="Eleanor of Arborea">
        Eleanor
       </a>
       , was written in this transitional variety of Sardinian, and remained in force until 1827.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is presumed the Arborean judges attempted to unify the two main Sardinian dialects in order to gain legitimacy and be therefore entitled to rule the entire island.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">
        Dante Alighieri
       </a>
       wrote in his 1302–05 essay
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/De_vulgari_eloquentia" title="De vulgari eloquentia">
         De vulgari eloquentia
        </a>
       </i>
       that
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sardinians" title="Sardinians">
        Sardinians
       </a>
       , not being
       <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">
        Italians
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Latii
       </i>
       ) and having no
       <i>
        lingua vulgaris
       </i>
       of their own, resorted to aping Latin instead.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_12-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Britannica-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dante_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dante-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salvi_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Salvi-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Dante's view has been dismissed, because Sardinian had evolved enough to be unintelligible to non-islanders. A popular 12th-century verse from the poem
       <i>
        Domna, tant vos ai preiada
       </i>
       quotes the
       <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">
        provençal
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Troubadour" title="Troubadour">
        troubadour
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Raimbaut_de_Vaqueiras" title="Raimbaut de Vaqueiras">
        Raimbaut de Vaqueiras
       </a>
       , saying
       <i>
        No t'intend plui d'un Toesco / o Sardo o Barbarì
       </i>
       ("I don't understand you any more than I understand a
       <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">
        German
       </a>
       / or a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sardinians" title="Sardinians">
        Sardinian
       </a>
       or a
       <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">
        Berber
       </a>
       ");
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salvi_55-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Salvi-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the Tuscan poet Fazio degli Uberti refers to the Sardinians in his poem
       <i>
        Dittamondo
       </i>
       as
       <i>
        una gente che niuno non la intende / né essi sanno quel ch'altri pispiglia
       </i>
       ("a people that no one is able to understand / nor do they come to a knowledge of what other peoples say").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dante_54-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dante-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salvi_55-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Salvi-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Geography_and_cartography_in_medieval_Islam" title="Geography and cartography in medieval Islam">
        Muslim geographer
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Idrisi" title="Muhammad al-Idrisi">
        Muhammad al-Idrisi
       </a>
       , who lived in
       <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">
        Palermo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">
        Sicily
       </a>
       at the court of King
       <a href="/wiki/Roger_II_of_Sicily" title="Roger II of Sicily">
        Roger II
       </a>
       , wrote in his work
       <i>
        Kitab nuzhat al-mushtaq fi'khtiraq al-'afaq
       </i>
       ("The book of pleasant journeys into faraway lands" or, simply, "The book of Roger") that "Sardinia is large, mountainous, poorly provided with water, two hundred and eighty miles long and one hundred and eighty long from west to east. [...] Sardinians are ethnically
       <i>
        Rūm Afāriqah
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Africans" title="Roman Africans">
        Latins of Africa
       </a>
       ), like the
       <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">
        Berbers
       </a>
       ; they shun contacts with all the other
       <i>
        Rūm
       </i>
       nations and are people of purpose and valiant that never leave the arms" (
       <i>
        Wa ahl Ğazīrat Sardāniya fī aṣl Rūm Afāriqa mutabarbirūn mutawaḥḥišūn min ağnās ar-Rūm wa hum ahl nağida wa hazm lā yufariqūn as-silāḥ
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statuti_Sassaresi_XIV_century_1a.png">
         <img alt="Two pages of an illuminated manuscript" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1959" data-file-width="3155" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Statuti_Sassaresi_XIV_century_1a.png/220px-Statuti_Sassaresi_XIV_century_1a.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Statuti_Sassaresi_XIV_century_1a.png/330px-Statuti_Sassaresi_XIV_century_1a.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Statuti_Sassaresi_XIV_century_1a.png/440px-Statuti_Sassaresi_XIV_century_1a.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statuti_Sassaresi_XIV_century_1a.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Sardinian-language statutes of
         <a href="/wiki/Sassari" title="Sassari">
          Sassari
         </a>
         from the 13th–14th centuries
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The literature of this period primarily consists of legal documents, besides the aforementioned Carta de Logu. The first document containing Sardinian elements is a 1063 donation to the abbey of Montecassino signed by Barisone I of Torres.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other documents are the
       <i>
        Carta Volgare
       </i>
       (1070–1080) in
       <a href="/wiki/Campidanese_dialect" title="Campidanese dialect">
        Campidanese
       </a>
       , the 1080 Logudorese Privilege,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the 1089 Donation of Torchitorio (in the
       <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">
        Marseille
       </a>
       archives),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the 1190–1206 Marsellaise Chart (in Campidanese)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and an 1173 communication between the Bishop Bernardo of
       <a href="/wiki/Olbia" title="Olbia">
        Civita
       </a>
       and Benedetto, who oversaw the Opera del Duomo in Pisa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Statutes of Sassari are written in
       <a href="/wiki/Logudorese_dialect" title="Logudorese dialect">
        Logudorese
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Aragonese_period_.E2.80.93_Catalan_influence">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Aragonese_period_–_Catalan_influence">
        Aragonese period –
        <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
         Catalan
        </a>
        influence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Aragonese period – Catalan influence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sardinia#Kingdom_of_the_Crown_of_Aragon" title="History of Sardinia">
        Kingdom of the Crown of Aragon
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The 1297
       <a href="/wiki/Feoffment" title="Feoffment">
        feoffment
       </a>
       of Sardinia by
       <a href="/wiki/Pope_Boniface_VIII" title="Pope Boniface VIII">
        Pope Boniface VIII
       </a>
       led to the creation of the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aragon" title="Kingdom of Aragon">
        Aragonese
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sardinia" title="Kingdom of Sardinia">
        Kingdom of Sardinia
       </a>
       and a long period of war between the Aragonese and Sardinians, ending with a
       <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">
        Catalan
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sanluri" title="Battle of Sanluri">
        victory at Sanluri
       </a>
       in 1409 and the renunciation of any succession right signed by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_III_of_Narbonne" title="William III of Narbonne">
        William III of Narbonne
       </a>
       in 1420.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During this period the clergy adopted
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       as their primary language, relegating Sardinian to a secondary status. According to attorney Sigismondo Arquer (Cagliari, 1530 – Toledo, 4 giugno 1571), author of
       <i>
        Sardiniae brevis historia et descriptio
       </i>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Sebastian_M%C3%BCnster" title="Sebastian Münster">
        Sebastian Münster
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Cosmographia_(Sebastian_M%C3%BCnster)" title="Cosmographia (Sebastian Münster)">
        Cosmographia Universalis
       </a>
       , Sardinian prevailed in rural areas and Catalan was spoken in the cities, where the ruling class eventually became bilingual in both languages;
       <a href="/wiki/Alghero" title="Alghero">
        Alghero
       </a>
       is still a Catalan-speaking enclave on Sardinia to this day.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The long-lasting war and the so-called
       <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">
        Black Death
       </a>
       had a devastating effect on the island, depopulating large parts of it. People from the neighbouring island of Corsica began to settle in the northern Sardinian coast, leading to the birth of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuscan_language" title="Tuscan language">
        Tuscan
       </a>
       -sounding
       <a href="/wiki/Sassarese_language" title="Sassarese language">
        Sassarese
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallurese_language" title="Gallurese language">
        Gallurese
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite Catalan being widely spoken and written on the island at this time (leaving a lasting influence in Sardinian), there are some written records of Sardinian. One is the 15th-century
       <i>
        Sa Vitta et sa Morte, et Passione de sanctu Gavinu, Brothu et Ianuariu
       </i>
       , written by Antòni Canu (1400–1476) and published in 1557:
       <i>
        Tando su rey barbaru su cane renegadu / de custa resposta multu restayt iradu / &amp; issu martiriu fetit apparigiare / itu su quale fesit fortemente ligare / sos sanctos martires cum bonas catenas / qui li segaant sos ossos cum sas veinas / &amp; totu sas carnes cum petenes de linu ...
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The 16th century is instead marked by a new Sardinian literary revival:
       <i>
        Rimas Spirituales
       </i>
       , by
       <a href="/wiki/Gerolamo_Araolla" title="Gerolamo Araolla">
        Hieronimu Araolla
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sardegnaculletorigini_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sardegnaculletorigini-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       was aimed at "glorifying and enriching Sardinian, our language" (
       <i>
        magnificare et arrichire sa limba nostra sarda
       </i>
       ) as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_people" title="Spanish people">
        Spanish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">
        French
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_people" title="Italian people">
        Italian
       </a>
       poets had already done for their languages (
       <i>
        la Deffense et illustration de la langue françoyse
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        il Dialogo delle lingue
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosita_28-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rosita-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Antonio Lo Frasso, a poet born in
       <a href="/wiki/Alghero" title="Alghero">
        Alghero
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arce_73-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Arce-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (a city he remembered fondly)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       who spent his life in
       <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">
        Barcelona
       </a>
       , wrote
       <a href="/wiki/Lyric_poetry" title="Lyric poetry">
        lyric poetry
       </a>
       in Sardinian:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ...
       <i>
        Non podende sufrire su tormentu / de su fogu ardente innamorosu. / Videndemi foras de sentimentu / et sensa una hora de riposu, / pensende istare liberu e contentu / m'agato pius aflitu e congoixosu, / in essermi de te senora apartadu, / mudende ateru quelu, ateru istadu ...
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Habsburg_period_.E2.80.93_Spanish_influence">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Habsburg_period_–_Spanish_influence">
        <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Spain" title="Habsburg Spain">
         Habsburg period
        </a>
        –
        <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
         Spanish
        </a>
        influence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Habsburg period – Spanish influence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Through the marriage of
       <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">
        Isabella I of Castile
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_Aragon" title="Ferdinand II of Aragon">
        Ferdinand II of Aragon
       </a>
       in 1469 and, later in 1624, the reorganization of the monarchy led by the
       <a href="/wiki/Gaspar_de_Guzm%C3%A1n,_Count-Duke_of_Olivares" title="Gaspar de Guzmán, Count-Duke of Olivares">
        Count-Duke of Olivares
       </a>
       , Sardinia would progressively join a broad Spanish cultural sphere and leave the exclusive
       <a href="/wiki/Aragon" title="Aragon">
        Aragonese
       </a>
       one. Spanish was perceived as an elitist language, gaining solid ground among the ruling Sardinian class; Spanish had thus a profound influence on Sardinian, especially in those words, styles and cultural models owing to the prestigious international role of the
       <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Monarchy" title="Habsburg Monarchy">
        Habsburg Monarchy
       </a>
       as well as the
       <a href="/wiki/Court_(royal)" title="Court (royal)">
        Court
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sardegnaculletorigini_71-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-sardegnaculletorigini-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most Sardinian authors would write in both Spanish and Sardinian until the 19th century and were well-versed in the former, so much so that
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vicente_Bacallar_y_Sanna" title="Vicente Bacallar y Sanna">
        Vicente Bacallar y Sanna
       </a>
       was one of the founders of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_Academia_Espa%C3%B1ola" title="Real Academia Española">
        Real Academia Española
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A notable exception was Pedro Delitala (1550–1590), who decided to write in Italian instead.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arce_73-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Arce-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nonetheless, the Sardinian language retained much of its importance, being the only one the people from rural areas kept speaking.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In "
       <i>
        Legendariu de Santas Virgines, et Martires de Iesu Christu
       </i>
       ", the
       <a href="/wiki/Orgosolo" title="Orgosolo">
        Orgolese
       </a>
       priest Ioan Matheu Garipa called Sardinian the closest living relative of
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">
        classical Latin
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        Las apo voltadas in sardu menjus qui non in atera limba pro amore de su vulgu [...] qui non tenjan bisonju de interprete pro bi-las decrarare, et tambene pro esser sa limba sarda tantu bona, quanta participat de sa latina, qui nexuna de quantas limbas si plàtican est tantu parente assa latina formale quantu sa sarda.
       </i>
      </p>
      <p>
       A 1620 proclamation is in the
       <a href="/wiki/Bosa" title="Bosa">
        Bosa
       </a>
       archives.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ploaghe,_camposanto,_lapidi_in_logudorese,_02.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1596" data-file-width="2538" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Ploaghe%2C_camposanto%2C_lapidi_in_logudorese%2C_02.JPG/250px-Ploaghe%2C_camposanto%2C_lapidi_in_logudorese%2C_02.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Ploaghe%2C_camposanto%2C_lapidi_in_logudorese%2C_02.JPG/375px-Ploaghe%2C_camposanto%2C_lapidi_in_logudorese%2C_02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Ploaghe%2C_camposanto%2C_lapidi_in_logudorese%2C_02.JPG/500px-Ploaghe%2C_camposanto%2C_lapidi_in_logudorese%2C_02.JPG 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ploaghe,_camposanto,_lapidi_in_logudorese,_02.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Some gravestones in the cemetery of
         <a href="/wiki/Ploaghe" title="Ploaghe">
          Ploaghe
         </a>
         . The first gravestone has writings in Sardinian, the other two in Italian.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Savoyard_period_and_Kingdom_of_Italy">
        Savoyard period and Kingdom of Italy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Savoyard period and Kingdom of Italy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sardinia#Piedmont-Sardinia" title="History of Sardinia">
        Piedmontese period
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Spanish_Succession" title="War of the Spanish Succession">
        War of the Spanish Succession
       </a>
       gave Sardinia to
       <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">
        Austria
       </a>
       , whose sovereignty was confirmed by the 1713–14 treaties of
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Utrecht" title="Treaty of Utrecht">
        Utrecht
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Rastatt" title="Treaty of Rastatt">
        Rastatt
       </a>
       . In 1717 a Spanish fleet
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Sardinia" title="Spanish conquest of Sardinia">
        reoccupied
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Cagliari" title="Cagliari">
        Cagliari
       </a>
       , and the following year Sardinia was ceded to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victor_Amadeus_II_of_Savoy" title="Victor Amadeus II of Savoy">
        Victor Amadeus II of Savoy
       </a>
       in exchange for
       <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">
        Sicily
       </a>
       . This transfer would not initially entail any social nor linguistic changes, though: Sardinia would still retain for a long time its Hispanic character, so much so that only in 1767 were the Aragonese and Spanish dynastic symbols replaced by the Savoyard cross.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the Savoyard period, a number of essays written by philologist Matteo Madau
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and professor (and senator)
       <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Spano" title="Giovanni Spano">
        Giovanni Spano
       </a>
       attempted to establish a unified
       <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">
        orthography
       </a>
       based on Logudorese, just like
       <a href="/wiki/Florentine_dialect" title="Florentine dialect">
        Florentine
       </a>
       would become the basis for Italian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1811, Vincenzo Raimondo Porru published the first essay on the Southern Sardinian grammar
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and in 1832 the first Sardinian-Italian dictionary as well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       However, the Savoyard government imposed Italian on Sardinia in July 1760,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       for reasons related more to the Savoyard need of drawing the island away from the Spanish influence than for Italian nationalism, which would be later pursued by the King
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Albert_of_Sardinia" title="Charles Albert of Sardinia">
        Charles Albert
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Spanish was thus replaced as the official language and Sardinian faced again marginalization.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The relationship between the newly imposed tongue and the native one had been immediately perceived by the locals, educated and uneducated alike, as a relationship (albeit unequal in terms of political power and prestige) between two very different languages, rather than between a language and one of its dialects like in other regions;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sardgruyter_95-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sardgruyter-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the plurisecular Iberian period contributed in making the Sardinians feel more detached from the Italian language and its cultural sphere, and the Spanish themselves, comprising both the Aragonese and Castilian ruling class, long considered Sardinian to be a distinct language with respect to their own tongues and Italian as well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sardgruyter_95-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-sardgruyter-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At the time, Italian was a foreign language to the Sardinians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Carlo Baudi di Vesme (
       <a href="/wiki/Cuneo" title="Cuneo">
        Cuneo
       </a>
       , 1809 –
       <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">
        Turin
       </a>
       , 1877) claimed that the suppression of Sardinian and the imposition of Italian was desirable in order to make the islanders "civilized" Italians,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       importing solely Italian-speaking teachers from other regions, and Piedmontese cartographers replaced many Sardinian place names with Italian ones. Despite the assimilation policy the anthem of the Savoyard
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Piedmont-Sardinia" title="Kingdom of Piedmont-Sardinia">
        Kingdom of Sardinia
       </a>
       was
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/S%27hymnu_sardu_nationale" title="S'hymnu sardu nationale">
         S'hymnu sardu nationale
        </a>
       </i>
       ("the Sardinian National Anthem"), also known as
       <i>
        Cunservet Deus su Re
       </i>
       ("God save the King"), with Sardinian lyrics first in Campidanese and then Logudorese.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the mobilization for
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
        World War I
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_Army" title="Italian Army">
        Italian Army
       </a>
       compelled all
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sardinians" title="Sardinians">
        Sardinians
       </a>
       to enlist as Italian subjects and established the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brigata_Sassari" title="Brigata Sassari">
        Sassari Infantry Brigade
       </a>
       on 1 March 1915 at
       <a href="/wiki/Tempio_Pausania" title="Tempio Pausania">
        Tempio Pausania
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sinnai" title="Sinnai">
        Sinnai
       </a>
       . Unlike the other infantry brigades of Italy, Sassari's conscripts were only Sardinians (including many officers). It is the only unit in Italy with an anthem in a language other than Italian:
       <i>
        Dimonios
       </i>
       ("Devils"), by Luciano Sechi. Its title derives from
       <i>
        Rote Teufel
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       for "red devils"). However,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compulsory_military_service" title="Compulsory military service">
        compulsory military service
       </a>
       played a role in language shift.
      </p>
      <p>
       Under
       <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">
        Fascism
       </a>
       , all languages other than Italian were banned, including Sardinia's improvised poetry competitions
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and a large number of Sardinian surnames were changed to sound more Italian (e.g.
       <i>
        Lussu
       </i>
       becoming
       <i>
        Lusso
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Pilu
       </i>
       changing to
       <i>
        Pilo
       </i>
       and so on). While this period saw the most aggressive cultural assimilation effort by the central government,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gruyter_102-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-gruyter-102">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the Sardinian Hymn of the Piedmontese Kingdom was a chance to use a regional language without penalty; as a royal tradition, it could not be forbidden.
      </p>
      <p>
       <span id="Current_situation">
       </span>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Present_situation">
        Present situation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Present situation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:No-smoking-sardinian.JPG">
         <img alt="Sign with graphic of crossed-out cigarette" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1512" data-file-width="2287" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/No-smoking-sardinian.JPG/220px-No-smoking-sardinian.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/No-smoking-sardinian.JPG/330px-No-smoking-sardinian.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/No-smoking-sardinian.JPG/440px-No-smoking-sardinian.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:No-smoking-sardinian.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Smoking_ban" title="Smoking ban">
          No-smoking sign
         </a>
         in Sardinian and
         <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
          Italian
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       After
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       , awareness around the Sardinian language and the danger of its slipping away did not seem to concern the Sardinian elites and entered the political spaces much later than in other European peripheries marked by the long-standing presence of ethno-linguistic minorities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
        <a href="#cite_note-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At the time of drafting of the statute in 1948, the legislator decided to specify the "Sardinian specialty" as a single criterion for political autonomy just on the grounds of a couple of socio-economic issues rather than considerations of a distinct cultural identity,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
        <a href="#cite_note-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       that were on the contrary looked down upon as a potential prelude to separatist claims.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
        <a href="#cite_note-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Eventually, the special statute of 1948 neither recognized any special territorial conditions about the region nor made any mention of a linguistic element. In the meantime, the emphasis on monolingual (Italian-only) policies and assimilation continued, with historical sites and ordinary objects renamed in Italian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
        <a href="#cite_note-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Public_Education_(Italy)" title="Ministry of Public Education (Italy)">
        Ministry of Public Education
       </a>
       reportedly requested that the Sardinian teachers be put under surveillance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
        <a href="#cite_note-108">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The rejection of the
       <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_language" title="Indigenous language">
        indigenous language
       </a>
       , along with a rigid model of Italian-language education,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
        <a href="#cite_note-109">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
       corporal punishment and shaming, led to poor schooling for Sardinians. Even now, Sardinia currently has the highest rate of school and university drop-out in Italy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
        <a href="#cite_note-110">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There have been many campaigns, often expressed in the form of political demands, to give Sardinian equal status with Italian as a means to promote cultural identity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
        <a href="#cite_note-111">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Following tensions and claims of the
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_nationalism" title="Sardinian nationalism">
        Sardinian nationalist movement
       </a>
       for concrete cultural and political autonomy, including the recognition of the Sardinians as an ethnic and linguistic minority, three separate bills were presented to the Regional Council in the '80s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosita_28-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rosita-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A survey conducted in 1984 (cited in Pinna Catte's work, 1992
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
        <a href="#cite_note-112">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) showed that many Sardinians had a positive attitude towards bilingual education (22% wanted Sardinian to be compulsory in Sardinian schools, while 54.7% would prefer to see teaching in Sardinian as optional); such consensus remains relatively stable to this day. A further survey in 2008 reported that more than half of the interviewees, 57.3%, are in favour of the introduction of Sardinian into schools alongside Italian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
        <a href="#cite_note-113">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the 1990s, Sardinian,
       <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">
        Albanian
       </a>
       , Catalan, German,
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Slovene_language" title="Slovene language">
        Slovenian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Croatian_language" title="Croatian language">
        Croatian
       </a>
       , French,
       <a href="/wiki/Franco-Proven%C3%A7al_language" title="Franco-Provençal language">
        Franco-Provençal
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Friulian_language" title="Friulian language">
        Friulian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ladin_language" title="Ladin language">
        Ladin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">
        Occitan
       </a>
       were formally recognized as
       <a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">
        minority languages
       </a>
       by ordinary Law no. 482/1999.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parl_114-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-parl-114">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nevertheless, in many Italian libraries and universities books about the Sardinian language are still classified as
       <i>
        Linguistica italiana
       </i>
       (Italian linguistics),
       <i>
        Dialetti italiani
       </i>
       (Italian dialects) or
       <i>
        Dialettologia italiana
       </i>
       (Italian dialectology).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
        <a href="#cite_note-115">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
        <a href="#cite_note-116">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sardinian is considered an "Italian dialect" by some
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
        <a href="#cite_note-117">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (even at the central institutional level
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
        <a href="#cite_note-118">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
        <a href="#cite_note-119">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) and it has been stigmatized as indicative of a lack of education;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tonzanu_120-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tonzanu-120">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
        <a href="#cite_note-121">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
