       it is still associated by many locals with shame, backwardness and provincialism.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
        <a href="#cite_note-122">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For instance, some Sardinians call the language
       <i>
        sa limba de su famine
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        sa lingua de su famini
       </i>
       , literally translating into English as "the language of hunger" (i.e. the language of the poor), regarding it as a stigma of a poverty-stricken, rural, and underprivileged life.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Segnaletica_bilingue_Sardegna.gif">
         <img alt="Bilingual sign pointing to a church" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="312" data-file-width="665" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Segnaletica_bilingue_Sardegna.gif/220px-Segnaletica_bilingue_Sardegna.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Segnaletica_bilingue_Sardegna.gif/330px-Segnaletica_bilingue_Sardegna.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Segnaletica_bilingue_Sardegna.gif/440px-Segnaletica_bilingue_Sardegna.gif 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Segnaletica_bilingue_Sardegna.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Bilingual Italian–Sardinian
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Road_sign" title="Road sign">
          road sign
         </a>
         in
         <a href="/wiki/Siniscola" title="Siniscola">
          Siniscola
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Besides, a number of other factors like a considerable immigration flow from mainland Italy, the interior
       <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">
        rural exodus
       </a>
       to urban areas, where Sardinian is spoken by a much lower percentage of the population,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
        <a href="#cite_note-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the use of Italian as a prerequisite for jobs and social advancement actually hinder any policy set up to promote the language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Euromosaic_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Euromosaic-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
        <a href="#cite_note-124">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
        <a href="#cite_note-125">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Therefore,
       <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">
        UNESCO
       </a>
       classifies Sardinian as "definitely
       <a href="/wiki/Endangered_language" title="Endangered language">
        endangered
       </a>
       ", because "many children learn the language, but some of them cease to use it throughout the school years".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
        <a href="#cite_note-126">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       At present, language use is far from stable:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosita_28-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rosita-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       reports show that, while an estimated 68 percent of the islanders have a good oral command of Sardinian, language ability among the children drops to around 13 percent, if not even less;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Euromosaic_17-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Euromosaic-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
        <a href="#cite_note-127">
         [127]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
        <a href="#cite_note-128">
         [128]
        </a>
       </sup>
       some linguists, like Mauro Maxia, cite the low number of Sardinian-speaking children as indicative of language decline, calling Sardinia "a case of linguistic suicide".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maxia_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Maxia-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Today, most people who speak Sardinian on an everyday basis live mainly in country-side areas, like the
       <a href="/wiki/Barbagia" title="Barbagia">
        Barbagia region
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
        <a href="#cite_note-129">
         [129]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
        <a href="#cite_note-130">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bill_(proposed_law)" title="Bill (proposed law)">
        bill
       </a>
       proposed by former prime minister
       <a href="/wiki/Mario_Monti" title="Mario Monti">
        Mario Monti
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Monti_Cabinet" title="Monti Cabinet">
        cabinet
       </a>
       would have lowered Sardinian's protection level,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
        <a href="#cite_note-131">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       distinguishing between languages protected by international agreements (German, Slovenian, French and Ladin) and
       <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_language" title="Indigenous language">
        indigenous languages
       </a>
       . This bill, which was not implemented (Italy, along with France and Malta,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
        <a href="#cite_note-132">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
       has signed but not
       <a href="/wiki/Ratification" title="Ratification">
        ratified
       </a>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">
        European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages
       </a>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
        <a href="#cite_note-133">
         [133]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
        <a href="#cite_note-134">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
       triggered a reaction on the island.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
        <a href="#cite_note-135">
         [135]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
        <a href="#cite_note-136">
         [136]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
        <a href="#cite_note-137">
         [137]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
        <a href="#cite_note-138">
         [138]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Students have expressed an interest in taking all (or part) of their exit examinations in Sardinian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
        <a href="#cite_note-139">
         [139]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
        <a href="#cite_note-140">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elisa_Melis_141-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Elisa_Melis-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
        <a href="#cite_note-142">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
        <a href="#cite_note-143">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
        <a href="#cite_note-144">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
        <a href="#cite_note-145">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
        <a href="#cite_note-146">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
        <a href="#cite_note-147">
         [147]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
        <a href="#cite_note-148">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Padre_Nostro_sardo.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2272" data-file-width="1704" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Padre_Nostro_sardo.jpg/220px-Padre_Nostro_sardo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Padre_Nostro_sardo.jpg/330px-Padre_Nostro_sardo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Padre_Nostro_sardo.jpg/440px-Padre_Nostro_sardo.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Padre_Nostro_sardo.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Pater_Noster" title="Church of the Pater Noster">
          Church of the Pater Noster
         </a>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
          Jerusalem
         </a>
         ),
         <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord's Prayer">
          Lord's Prayer
         </a>
         plaque in Sardinian
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In response to a 2013 Italian initiative to remove bilingual signs, a group of Sardinians began a virtual campaign on
       <a href="/wiki/Google_Maps" title="Google Maps">
        Google Maps
       </a>
       to replace Italian place names with the original Sardinian names. After about one month, Google changed the place names back to Italian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
        <a href="#cite_note-149">
         [149]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
        <a href="#cite_note-150">
         [150]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
        <a href="#cite_note-151">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After a signature campaign,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
        <a href="#cite_note-152">
         [152]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it has been made possible to change the language setting on
       <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">
        Facebook
       </a>
       from any language to Sardinian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
        <a href="#cite_note-153">
         [153]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
        <a href="#cite_note-154">
         [154]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
        <a href="#cite_note-155">
         [155]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
        <a href="#cite_note-156">
         [156]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is also possible to switch to Sardinian even in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telegram" title="Telegram">
        Telegram
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
        <a href="#cite_note-157">
         [157]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
        <a href="#cite_note-158">
         [158]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and a couple of other apps, like
       <a href="/wiki/Vivaldi_(web_browser)" title="Vivaldi (web browser)">
        Vivaldi
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/F-Droid" title="F-Droid">
        F-Droid
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Diaspora_(social_network)" title="Diaspora (social network)">
        Diaspora
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/OsmAnd" title="OsmAnd">
        OsmAnd
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Notepad%2B%2B" title="Notepad++">
        Notepad++
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swiftkey" title="Swiftkey">
        Swiftkey
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Stellarium_(software)" title="Stellarium (software)">
        Stellarium
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
        <a href="#cite_note-159">
         [159]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Skype" title="Skype">
        Skype
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
        <a href="#cite_note-160">
         [160]
        </a>
       </sup>
       etc. In 2016, the first automatic translation software from Italian to Sardinian was developed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
        <a href="#cite_note-161">
         [161]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the 1990s, there was a resurgence of Sardinian-language music, ranging from the more
       <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Sardinia" title="Music of Sardinia">
        traditional genres
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cantu_a_tenore" title="Cantu a tenore">
         cantu a tenore
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cantu_a_chiterra" title="Cantu a chiterra">
         cantu a chiterra
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        gozos
       </i>
       etc.) to
       <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">
        rock
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Kenze Neke
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Askra
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Tzoku
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tazenda" title="Tazenda">
         Tazenda
        </a>
       </i>
       etc.) and even
       <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">
        hip hop
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rap_music" title="Rap music">
        rap
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Dr. Drer e CRC Posse
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Quilo
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Sa Razza
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Malam
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Menhir
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Stranos Elementos
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Randagiu Sardu
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Futta
       </i>
       etc.), and with artists who use the language as a means to promote the island and address its long-standing issues and the new challenges.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
        <a href="#cite_note-162">
         [162]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">
        <a href="#cite_note-163">
         [163]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164">
        <a href="#cite_note-164">
         [164]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
        <a href="#cite_note-165">
         [165]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are also a few films (like
       <i>
        Su Re
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Bellas Mariposas
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Treulababbu
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Sonetaula
       </i>
       etc.) dubbed in Sardinian,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
        <a href="#cite_note-166">
         [166]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and some others (like
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolis_(1927_film)" title="Metropolis (1927 film)">
        Metropolis
       </a>
       ) provided with subtitles in the language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">
        <a href="#cite_note-167">
         [167]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2015, all the political parties in the Sardinian regional council have reached an agreement involving a series of amendments to the old 1997 law in order to introduce the optional teaching of the language in Sardinia's schools;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
        <a href="#cite_note-168">
         [168]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
        <a href="#cite_note-169">
         [169]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170">
        <a href="#cite_note-170">
         [170]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as of now, this law has yet to be approved. Although there is still not an option to teach Sardinian on the island itself, let alone in Italy, some language courses are instead sometimes available in
       <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
        Germany
       </a>
       (Universities of
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Stuttgart" title="University of Stuttgart">
        Stuttgart
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Maximilian_University_of_Munich" title="Ludwig Maximilian University of Munich">
        Munich
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_T%C3%BCbingen" title="University of Tübingen">
        Tübingen
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Mannheim" title="University of Mannheim">
        Mannheim
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171">
        <a href="#cite_note-171">
         [171]
        </a>
       </sup>
       etc.),
       <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
        Spain
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Girona" title="University of Girona">
        University of Girona
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
        <a href="#cite_note-172">
         [172]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">
        Czech Republic
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Brno" title="Brno">
        Brno
       </a>
       university).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
        <a href="#cite_note-173">
         [173]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
        <a href="#cite_note-174">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Shigeaki Sugeta also taught Sardinian to his students of Romance languages at the
       <a href="/wiki/Waseda_University" title="Waseda University">
        Waseda University
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">
        Tokyo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">
        Japan
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175">
        <a href="#cite_note-175">
         [175]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176">
        <a href="#cite_note-176">
         [176]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
        <a href="#cite_note-177">
         [177]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178">
        <a href="#cite_note-178">
         [178]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Minoranze_linguistiche_it.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5122" data-file-width="4022" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Minoranze_linguistiche_it.svg/200px-Minoranze_linguistiche_it.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Minoranze_linguistiche_it.svg/300px-Minoranze_linguistiche_it.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Minoranze_linguistiche_it.svg/400px-Minoranze_linguistiche_it.svg.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Minoranze_linguistiche_it.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Sardinian-speaking community among the other minority language groups officially recognized by Italy.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179">
          <a href="#cite_note-179">
           [179]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       At present, the Sardinian-speaking community is the least protected one in Italy, despite being the largest minority language group officially recognized by the state.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ministero_29-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ministero-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
        <a href="#cite_note-180">
         [180]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In fact the language, which is receding in all domains of use, is still not given access to any field of public life,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Euromosaic_17-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Euromosaic-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       such as education (Italian–Sardinian bilingualism is still frowned upon,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maxia_16-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Maxia-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elisa_Melis_141-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Elisa_Melis-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181">
        <a href="#cite_note-181">
         [181]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182">
        <a href="#cite_note-182">
         [182]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while the local universities do not play pretty much any role whatsoever in supporting the language
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuttgart_183-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stuttgart-183">
         [183]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184">
        <a href="#cite_note-184">
         [184]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185">
        <a href="#cite_note-185">
         [185]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ), politics (with the exception of some nationalist groups
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186">
        <a href="#cite_note-186">
         [186]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ), justice, administrative authorities and public services, media,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187">
        <a href="#cite_note-187">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188">
        <a href="#cite_note-188">
         [188]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189">
        <a href="#cite_note-189">
         [189]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and cultural,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190">
        <a href="#cite_note-190">
         [190]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ecclesiastical,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191">
        <a href="#cite_note-191">
         [191]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192">
        <a href="#cite_note-192">
         [192]
        </a>
       </sup>
       economic and social activities, as well as facilities. According to a 2017 report on the digital language diversity in Europe, Sardinian appears to be particularly vital on social media as part of many people's everyday life for private use, but such vitality does not still translate into a strong and wide availability of Internet media for the language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193">
        <a href="#cite_note-193">
         [193]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2017, a 60-hour Sardinian language course has been introduced for the first time in Sardinia and Italy at the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cagliari" title="University of Cagliari">
        University of Cagliari
       </a>
       , although such a course was already available in other universities abroad.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194">
        <a href="#cite_note-194">
         [194]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2015, the
       <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">
        Council of Europe
       </a>
       commented on the status of national minorities in Italy, noting the
       <i>
        à la carte
       </i>
       approach of the Italian state towards them with the exception of the German, French and Slovenian languages, where Italy has applied full bilingualism due to international agreements. Despite the formal recognition from the Italian state, Italy does not in fact collect any information on the ethnic and linguistic composition of the population, apart from
       <a href="/wiki/South_Tyrol" title="South Tyrol">
        South Tyrol
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195">
        <a href="#cite_note-195">
         [195]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is also virtually no print and broadcasting media exposure in politically or numerically weaker minorites like Sardinian. Moreover, the resources allocated to cultural projects like bilingual education, which lacks a consistent approach, are largely insufficient to meet "even the most basic expectations".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196">
        <a href="#cite_note-196">
         [196]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197">
        <a href="#cite_note-197">
         [197]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198">
        <a href="#cite_note-198">
         [198]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199">
        <a href="#cite_note-199">
         [199]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       With things being the way they are now, Sardinian is considered a low means of communication
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Euromosaic_17-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Euromosaic-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       relegated to little more than highly localised levels of interaction.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Euromosaic_17-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Euromosaic-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With a solution to the Sardinian question being unlikely to be found anytime soon,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosita_28-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rosita-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the language has become highly endangered.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuttgart_183-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stuttgart-183">
         [183]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most of the younger generation, although they do understand some Sardinian, is in fact Italian
       <a href="/wiki/Monolingualism" title="Monolingualism">
        monolingual
       </a>
       and monocultural,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Euromosaic_17-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Euromosaic-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       speaking a
       <a href="/wiki/Regional_Italian#Sardinia" title="Regional Italian">
        Sardinian-influenced dialect of Italian
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200">
        <a href="#cite_note-200">
         [200]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosita_28-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rosita-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201">
        <a href="#cite_note-201">
         [201]
        </a>
       </sup>
       that is often nicknamed
       <i>
        italiànu porcheddìnu
       </i>
       ("pig Italian", meaning more or less "broken Italian") by many native Sardinian speakers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202">
        <a href="#cite_note-202">
         [202]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Whatever the fate of the receding Sardinian language might be, it shall constitute the substratum of the prevailing one (Italian) in a number of linguistic components.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       All dialects of Sardinian have phonetic features that are relatively archaic compared to other
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       . The degree of archaism varies, with the dialect spoken in the
       <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Nuoro" title="Province of Nuoro">
        Province of Nuoro
       </a>
       being considered the most conservative. Medieval evidence indicates that the language spoken on
       <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">
        Sardinia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">
        Corsica
       </a>
       at the time was similar to modern Nuorese Sardinian. The other dialects are thought to have evolved through Catalan, Spanish and later Italian influences.
      </p>
      <p>
       The examples listed below are from the
       <a href="/wiki/Logudorese_dialect" title="Logudorese dialect">
        Logudorese dialect
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
         Latin
        </a>
        short vowels
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [i]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [u]
        </span>
        have preserved their original sound; in
        <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
         Italian
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
         Spanish
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
         Portuguese
        </a>
        they became
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [e]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [o]
        </span>
        , respectively (for example,
        <i>
         siccus
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         sicu
        </i>
        , "dry" (Italian
        <i>
         secco
        </i>
        , Spanish and Portuguese
        <i>
         seco
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        Preservation of the plosive sounds
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [k]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɡ]
        </span>
        before front vowels
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [e]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [i]
        </span>
        in many words; for example,
        <i>
         centum
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         kentu
        </i>
        , "hundred";
        <i>
         decem
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         dèke
        </i>
        , "ten" and
        <i>
         gener
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         gheneru
        </i>
        , "son-in-law" (Italian
        <i>
         cento, dièci, genero
        </i>
        with
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [tʃ]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [dʒ]
        </span>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        Absence of
        <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
         diphthongizations
        </a>
        found in other Romance languages; for example,
        <i>
         potest
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         podest
        </i>
        , "he can" (Italian
        <i>
         può
        </i>
        , Spanish
        <i>
         puede
        </i>
        , Portuguese
        <i>
         pode
        </i>
        );
        <i>
         bonus
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         bónu
        </i>
        , "good" (Italian
        <i>
         buono
        </i>
        , Spanish
        <i>
         bueno
        </i>
        , Portuguese
        <i>
         bom
        </i>
        )
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Sardinian contains the following phonetic innovations:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Change of the Latin -
        <i>
         ll
        </i>
        - into a
        <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_consonant" title="Retroflex consonant">
         retroflex
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɖɖ]
        </span>
        , shared with
        <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_language" title="Sicilian language">
         Sicilian
        </a>
        and Southern
        <a href="/wiki/Corsican_language" title="Corsican language">
         Corsican
        </a>
        ; for example,
        <i>
         corallus
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         coraddu
        </i>
        , "coral" and
        <i>
         villa
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         bidda
        </i>
        , "village, town"
       </li>
       <li>
        Similar changes in the consonant clusters -
        <i>
         ld
        </i>
        - and -
        <i>
         nd
        </i>
        -:
        <i>
         soldus
        </i>
        &gt;
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈsoɖ.ɖu]
        </span>
        (money),
        <i>
         abundantia
        </i>
        &gt;
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ab.buɳ.ˈɖan.tsi.a]
        </span>
        (abundance)
       </li>
       <li>
        Evolution of
        <i>
         pl
        </i>
        -,
        <i>
         fl
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         cl
        </i>
        into
        <i>
         pr
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         fr
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         cr
        </i>
        , as in Portuguese and
        <a href="/wiki/Galician_language" title="Galician language">
         Galician
        </a>
        ; for example,
        <i>
         platea
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         pratza
        </i>
        , "plaza" (Portuguese
        <i>
         praça
        </i>
        , Galician
        <i>
         praza
        </i>
        , Italian
        <i>
         piazza
        </i>
        ),
        <i>
         fluxus
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         frúsciu
        </i>
        , "flabby" (Portuguese and Galician
        <i>
         frouxo
        </i>
        ) and
        <i>
         ecclesia
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         cresia
        </i>
        , "church" (Portuguese
        <i>
         igreja
        </i>
        , Galician
        <i>
         igrexa
        </i>
        and Italian
        <i>
         chiesa
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Metathesis_(linguistics)" title="Metathesis (linguistics)">
         Metathesis
        </a>
        such as
        <i>
         abb
         <b>
          r
         </b>
         atzare
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         abba
         <b>
          l
         </b>
         tzare
        </i>
        (to embrace)
       </li>
       <li>
        Vowel
        <a href="/wiki/Prothesis_(linguistics)" title="Prothesis (linguistics)">
         prothesis
        </a>
        before an initial
        <i>
         r
        </i>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Campidanese_dialect" title="Campidanese dialect">
         Campidanese
        </a>
        , similar to
        <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">
         Basque
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Gascon_language" title="Gascon language">
         Gascon
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         rex
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         urrei
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         re
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         gurrèi
        </i>
        (king);
        <i>
         rota
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         arroda
        </i>
        (wheel) (Gascon
        <i>
         arròda
        </i>
        );
        <i>
         rivus
        </i>
        &gt; Sardinian and Gascon
        <i>
         arríu
        </i>
        (river)
       </li>
       <li>
        Vowel prothesis in Logudorese before an initial
        <i>
         s
        </i>
        followed by consonant, as in the
        <a href="/wiki/Western_Romance_languages" title="Western Romance languages">
         Western Romance languages
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         scriptum
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         iscrítu
        </i>
        (Spanish
        <i>
         escrito
        </i>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
         French
        </a>
        <i>
         écrit
        </i>
        ),
        <i>
         stella
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         isteddu
        </i>
        , "star" (Spanish
        <i>
         estrella
        </i>
        , French
        <i>
         étoile
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Except for the Nuorese dialects,
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/intervocalic" title="wiktionary:intervocalic">
         intervocalic
        </a>
        Latin single voiceless plosives
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [p, t, k]
        </span>
        became voiced
        <a href="/wiki/Approximant_consonant" title="Approximant consonant">
         approximant consonants
        </a>
        . Single voiced plosives
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [b, d, ɡ]
        </span>
        were lost:
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [t]
        </span>
        &gt;
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [d]
        </span>
        (or its soft counterpart,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ð]
        </span>
        ):
        <i>
         locus
        </i>
        &gt;
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ˈlo.ɡu]
        </span>
        (Italian
        <i>
         luògo
        </i>
        ),
        <i>
         caritas
        </i>
        &gt;
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         ['ka.ri.tas]
        </span>
        (It.
        <i>
         carità
        </i>
        ). This also applies across word boundaries:
        <i>
         porcus
        </i>
        (pig), but
        <i>
         su borku
        </i>
        (the pig);
        <i>
         domu
        </i>
        (house), but
        <i>
         sa omu
        </i>
        (the house). Such sound changes have become grammaticalised, making Sardinian an initial mutating language with similarities in this to the Celtic Languages.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Although the latter two features were acquired during Spanish rule, the others indicate a deeper relationship between ancient Sardinia and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">
        Iberian
       </a>
       world; the retroflex
       <i>
        d
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        l
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        r
       </i>
       are found in southern Italy,
       <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">
        Tuscany
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Asturias" title="Asturias">
        Asturias
       </a>
       , and were probably involved in the palatalization process of the Latin clusters -
       <i>
        ll
       </i>
       -,
       <i>
        pl
       </i>
       -,
       <i>
        cl
       </i>
       - (-
       <i>
        ll
       </i>
       - &gt;
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       and Catalan -
       <i>
        ll
       </i>
       -
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʎ]
       </span>
       , Gascon -
       <i>
        th
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [c]
       </span>
       ;
       <i>
        cl
       </i>
       - &gt;
       <a href="/wiki/Galician-Portuguese" title="Galician-Portuguese">
        Galician-Portuguese
       </a>
       <i>
        ch
       </i>
       -
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tʃ]
       </span>
       , Ital.
       <i>
        chi
       </i>
       -
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kj]
       </span>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/Eduardo_Blasco_Ferrer" title="Eduardo Blasco Ferrer">
        Eduardo Blasco Ferrer
       </a>
       , Sardinian has the following phonemes:
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">
        Vowels
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Vowels">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The five vowels
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a/ /e/ /i/ /o/ /u/
       </span>
       , without length differentiation. There are also nasal vowels [ã], [ẽ], [ĩ], [õ], [ũ] in some varieties, and even nasal diphthongs when an intervocalic -n- is deleted like in "beni" [bẽj̃~bẽĩ].
      </p>

          </a>

         </a>

        </td>
        <td>

        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Approximant_consonant" title="Approximant consonant">
          Approximant
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          <a href="/wiki/Palatal_approximant#Palatal" title="Palatal approximant">
           j
          </a>
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       There are three series of plosives or corresponding approximants:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Voiceless stops derive from their Latin counterparts in composition after another stop. They are reinforced (
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geminate_consonant" title="Geminate consonant">
         double
        </a>
        ) in initial position, but this reinforcement is not written because it does not produce a different
        <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">
         phoneme
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Double voiced stops (after another consonant) derive from their Latin equivalents in composition after another stop.
       </li>
       <li>
        Weak voiced "stops", sometimes transcribed ⟨β, δ, ğ⟩ (approximants
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [β, ð, ɣ]
        </span>
        after vowels, as in Spanish), derive from single Latin stops (voiced or voiceless).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Cagliari" title="Cagliari">
        Cagliari
       </a>
       and neighboring dialects, the soft
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [d]
       </span>
       is assimilated to the
       <a href="/wiki/Rhotic_consonant" title="Rhotic consonant">
        rhotic consonant
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɾ]
       </span>
       :
       <i>
        digitus
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        didu
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        diru
       </i>
       (finger).
      </p>
      <p>
       The double-
       <a href="/wiki/Voiced_retroflex_stop" title="Voiced retroflex stop">
        voiced retroflex stop
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɖɖ/
       </span>
       (written
       <i>
        dd
       </i>
       ) derives from the former
       <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_lateral_approximant" title="Retroflex lateral approximant">
        retroflex lateral approximant
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɭɭ/
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Fricatives">
        Fricatives
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Fricatives">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Labiodental_consonant" title="Labiodental consonant">
         labiodentals
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /f/
        </span>
        (sometimes pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ff]
        </span>
        or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [v]
        </span>
        in initial position) and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /v/
        </span>
        <ul>
         <li>
          Latin initial
          <i>
           v
          </i>
          becomes
          <i>
           b
          </i>
          (
          <i>
           vipera
          </i>
          &gt;
          <i>
           bibera
          </i>
          , "viper")
          <ul>
           <li>
            In central Sardinia the sound
            <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
             /f/
            </span>
            disappears, akin to the
            <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
             /f/
            </span>
            &gt;
            <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
             /h/
            </span>
            change in Gascon and Spanish.
           </li>
          </ul>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [θ]
        </span>
        , written
        <i>
         th
        </i>
        (as in the English
        <i>
         thing
        </i>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative">
         voiceless dental fricative
        </a>
        ), is a restricted dialectal variety of the phoneme
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ts/
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /s/
        </span>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ss/
        </span>
        : For example,
        <i>
         ipsa
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         íssa
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʃ/
        </span>
        : Pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʃ]
        </span>
        at the beginning of a word, otherwise
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʃʃ]
        </span>
        =
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʃ.ʃ]
        </span>
        , and is written
        <i>
         sc(i/e)
        </i>
        . The voiced equivalent,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʒ]
        </span>
        , is often spelled with the letter
        <i>
         x
        </i>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Affricates">
        Affricates
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Affricates">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ts/
        </span>
        (or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [tts]
        </span>
        ), a
        <a href="/wiki/Denti-alveolar_consonant" title="Denti-alveolar consonant">
         denti-alveolar
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Affricate_consonant" title="Affricate consonant">
         affricate consonant
        </a>
        written
        <i>
         tz
        </i>
        , corresponds to Italian
        <i>
         z
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         ci
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /dz/
        </span>
        (or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ddz]
        </span>
        ), written
        <i>
         z
        </i>
        , corresponds to Italian
        <i>
         gi
        </i>
        - or
        <i>
         ggi
        </i>
        - respectively.
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tʃ/
        </span>
        , written
        <i>
         c(i/e)
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         ç
        </i>
        (also written
        <i>
         ts
        </i>
        in loanwords)
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ttʃ/
        </span>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /dʒ/
        </span>
        , written
        <i>
         g(e/i)
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         j
        </i>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Nasals">
        Nasals
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Nasals">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /m/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /mm/
        </span>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /n/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /nn/
        </span>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɲɲ/
        </span>
        , written
        <i>
         gn
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203">
         <a href="#cite_note-203">
          [203]
         </a>
        </sup>
        or "nny"/"nni"
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204">
         <a href="#cite_note-204">
          [204]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (the
        <a href="/wiki/Palatal_nasal" title="Palatal nasal">
         palatal nasal
        </a>
        for some speakers or dialects, although for most the pronunciation is
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [nːj]
        </span>
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
        )
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Liquids">
        Liquids
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Liquids">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /l/
        </span>
        (or
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ll]
        </span>
        ), double initially
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɾ/
        </span>
        , a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_flap" title="Alveolar flap">
         flap
        </a>
        written
        <i>
         r
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /r/
        </span>
        , a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
         trill
        </a>
        written
        <i>
         rr
        </i>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Some permutations of
       <i>
        l
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        r
       </i>
       are seen; in most dialects, a preconsonant
       <i>
        l
       </i>
       (for example,
       <i>
        lt
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        lc
       </i>
       ) becomes
       <i>
        r
       </i>
       : Latin "altum" &gt;
       <i>
        artu
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        marralzu
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        marrarzu
       </i>
       , "rock".
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Palatal_consonant" title="Palatal consonant">
        palatal
       </a>
       context, Latin
       <i>
        l
       </i>
       changed into
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [dz]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ts]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ldz]
       </span>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ll]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [dʒ]
       </span>
       , rather than the
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʎ]
       </span>
       of Italian:
       <i>
        achizare
       </i>
       (Italian
       <i>
        accigliare
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        *volia
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        bòlla
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        bòlza
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        bòza
       </i>
       , "wish" (Italian
       <i>
        vòglia
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        folia
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        fogia
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        folla
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        foza
       </i>
       , "leaf" (Italian
       <i>
        foglia
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        filia
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        filla
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        fidza
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        fiza
       </i>
       , "daughter" (Italian
       <i>
        figlia
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">
        Grammar
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Grammar">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Sardinian's distinctive features are:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The plural
        <a href="/wiki/Marker_(linguistics)" title="Marker (linguistics)">
         marker
        </a>
        is
        <i>
         -s
        </i>
        (from the Latin
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accusative" title="Accusative">
         accusative
        </a>
        plural), as in the Western Romance languages French,
        <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">
         Occitan
        </a>
        , Catalan, Spanish,
        <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
         Portuguese
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Galician_language" title="Galician language">
         Galician
        </a>
        ):
        <i>
         sardu, sardus
        </i>
        , "sardinian";
        <i>
         pudda
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         puddas
        </i>
        , "hen";
        <i>
         margiane, margianes
        </i>
        , "fox". In Italo-Dalmatian languages (such as Italian) or
        <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Romance_languages" title="Eastern Romance languages">
         Eastern Romance
        </a>
        languages (such as
        <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">
         Romanian
        </a>
        ), the plural ends with
        <i>
         -i
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         -e
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Sardinian uses a definite article derived from the Latin
        <i>
         ipse
        </i>
        :
        <i>
         su, sa
        </i>
        , plural
        <i>
         sos, sas
        </i>
        (Logudorese) and
        <i>
         is
        </i>
        (Campidanese). Such articles are common in
        <a href="/wiki/Balearic_dialect" title="Balearic dialect">
         Balearic Catalan
        </a>
        , and were common in
        <a href="/wiki/Gascon_language" title="Gascon language">
         Gascon
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        A
        <a href="/wiki/Periphrasis" title="Periphrasis">
         periphrastic construction
        </a>
        of "have to" (late Latin
        <i>
         habere ad
        </i>
        ) is used for the future:
        <i>
         ap'a istàre
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         apo a istàre
        </i>
        "I will stay", Vulgar Latin 'habeo ad stare' (as in the Portuguese
        <i>
         hei de estar
        </i>
        , but here as periphrasis for
        <i>
         estarei
        </i>
        ). Other Latin languages have realisations of the alternative Vulgar Latin 'stare habeo', Italian "starò", Spanish "estaré".
       </li>
       <li>
        For prohibitions, a negative form of the subjunctive is used:
        <i>
         no bengias!
        </i>
        , "don't come!" (compare Spanish
        <i>
         no vengas
        </i>
        and Portuguese
        <i>
         não venhas
        </i>
        , classified as part of the affirmative
        <a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">
         imperative mood
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">
        Varieties
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Varieties">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Logudorese_dialect" title="Logudorese dialect">
        Logudorese dialect
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Campidanese_dialect" title="Campidanese dialect">
        Campidanese dialect
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gallurese.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="721" data-file-width="1105" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Gallurese.png/220px-Gallurese.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Gallurese.png/330px-Gallurese.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Gallurese.png/440px-Gallurese.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gallurese.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Sassarese (orange) and Gallurese (yellow) with respect to Sardinian (green)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Historically, the Sardinian population has always been quite small and scattered across isolated
       <a href="/wiki/Canton_(country_subdivision)" title="Canton (country subdivision)">
        cantons
       </a>
       , sharing similar demographic patterns with the Corsican one. The Sardinian language is traditionally subdivided by many scholars into two macro-varieties, each spoken by roughly half of the entire community with neither having the edge over the other: the
       <a href="/wiki/Logudorese_dialect" title="Logudorese dialect">
        Logudorese
       </a>
       dialects (
       <i>
        su sardu logudoresu
       </i>
       ) in North Central Sardinia, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Campidanese_dialect" title="Campidanese dialect">
        Campidanese
       </a>
       dialects (
       <i>
        su sardu campidanesu
       </i>
       ) in South Central Sardinia. They differ primarily in phonetics, which does not hamper intelligibility.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sardinianvarieties_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sardinianvarieties-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Logudorese is considered the more conservative dialect, with the Nuorese
       <a href="/wiki/Subdialect" title="Subdialect">
        subdialect
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        su sardu nugoresu
       </i>
       ) being the most conservative of all. It has retained the
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">
        classical Latin
       </a>
       pronunciation of the stop velars (
       <i>
        kena
       </i>
       versus
       <i>
        cena
       </i>
       , "supper"), the front middle vowels (compare Campidanese
       <a href="/wiki/Iotacism" title="Iotacism">
        iotacism
       </a>
       , probably from Byzantine Greek)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205">
        <a href="#cite_note-205">
         [205]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and assimilation of
       <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_vowel" title="Close-mid vowel">
        close-mid vowels
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        cane
       </i>
       versus
       <i>
        cani
       </i>
       , "dog" and
       <i>
        gattos
       </i>
       versus
       <i>
        gattus
       </i>
       , "cats"). Labio-velars become plain labials (
       <i>
        limba
       </i>
       versus
       <i>
        lingua
       </i>
       , "language" and
       <i>
        abba
       </i>
       versus
       <i>
        acua
       </i>
       , "water").
       <i>
        I
       </i>
       is prosthesized before consonant clusters beginning in
       <i>
        s
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        iscala
       </i>
       versus Campidanese
       <i>
        scala
       </i>
       , "stairway" and
       <i>
        iscola
       </i>
       versus
       <i>
        scola
       </i>
       , "school"). An east-west strip of small villages in central Sardinia speaks a transitional group of dialects (
       <i>
        su sardu de mesania
       </i>
       ). Examples include
       <i>
        is limbas
       </i>
       (the languages) and
       <i>
        is abbas
       </i>
       (the waters). Campidanese is the dialect spoken in the southern half of Sardinia (including
       <a href="/wiki/Cagliari" title="Cagliari">
        Cagliari
       </a>
       , once the metropolis of the Roman province), influenced by Rome,
       <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">
        Carthage
       </a>
       , Constantinople and
       <a href="/wiki/Late_Latin" title="Late Latin">
        Late Latin
       </a>
       . Examples include
       <i>
        is fruminis
       </i>
       (the rivers) and
       <i>
        is domus
       </i>
       (the houses).
      </p>
      <p>
       Sardinian is the language of most Sardinian communities. However, Sardinian is not spoken as the native and primary language in a significant number of Sardinian communities (amounting to 20% of the Sardinian population).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ministero_29-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ministero-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sardinianvarieties_6-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sardinianvarieties-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Two Sardinian–
       <a href="/wiki/Corsican_language" title="Corsican language">
        Corsican
       </a>
       transitional languages (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallurese_language" title="Gallurese language">
        Gallurese
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sassarese_language" title="Sassarese language">
        Sassarese
       </a>
       ) are spoken in the northernmost part of Sardinia,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minoranze_206-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-minoranze-206">
         [206]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207">
        <a href="#cite_note-207">
         [207]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although some Sardinian is also understood by the majority of people living there (73,6% in
       <a href="/wiki/Gallura" title="Gallura">
        Gallura
       </a>
       and 67,8% in the Sassarese-speaking subregion).
       <a href="/wiki/Sassari" title="Sassari">
        Sassari
       </a>
       , the second-largest city on Sardinia and the main center of the northern half of the island (
       <i>
        cabu de susu
       </i>
       in Sardinian,
       <i>
        capo di sopra
       </i>
       in Italian), is located there. There are also two
       <a href="/wiki/Language_island" title="Language island">
        language islands
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">
        Catalan
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Algherese_dialect" title="Algherese dialect">
        Algherese
       </a>
       -speaking community from the inner city of
       <a href="/wiki/Alghero" title="Alghero">
        Alghero
       </a>
       (northwest Sardinia) and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligurian_language_(Romance)" title="Ligurian language (Romance)">
        Ligurian
       </a>
       -speaking towns of
       <a href="/wiki/Carloforte" title="Carloforte">
        Carloforte
       </a>
       , in
       <a href="/wiki/San_Pietro_Island" title="San Pietro Island">
        San Pietro Island
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Calasetta" title="Calasetta">
        Calasetta
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Sant%27Antioco" title="Sant'Antioco">
        Sant'Antioco island
       </a>
       (south-west Sardinia).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minoranze_206-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-minoranze-206">
         [206]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_of_text">
        Sample of text
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sardinian_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Sample of text">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="itwiki_template_toc" style="text-align:center" width="100%">
       <tr>
        <th bgcolor="#EFEFEF">
         English
        </th>
        <th bgcolor="#EFEFEF">
         Logudorese Sardinian
        </th>
        <th bgcolor="#EFEFEF">
         Campidanese Sardinian
        </th>
        <th bgcolor="#EFEFEF">
         Transitional Mesanìa dialect
        </th>
        <th bgcolor="#EFEFEF">
         Latin
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <p>
          Our Father in heaven,
          <br/>
          hallowed be Your name.
          <br/>
          Your kingdom come,
          <br/>
          Your will be done,
          <br/>
          on earth as it is in heaven.
          <br/>
          Give us this day our daily bread,
          <br/>
          and forgive us our debts,
          <br/>
          as we also have forgiven our debtors.
          <br/>
          And lead us not into temptation,
          <br/>
          but deliver us from evil.
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          Babbu nostru chi ses in chelu,
          <br/>
          Santificadu siat su nomine tou.
          <br/>
          Benzat a nois su rennu tou,
          <br/>
          Siat fatta sa boluntade tua,
          <br/>
          comente in chelu gai in terra.
          <br/>
          Dona nos oe su pane nostru de donzi die,
          <br/>
          E perdona nos sos peccados nostros,
          <br/>
          Comente nois perdonamus a sos depidores nostros.
          <br/>
          E no nos lesses ruer in tentatzione,
          <br/>
          E libera nos dae male.
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          Babbu nostu chi ses in celu,
          <br/>
          Santificau siat su nomini tuu.
          <br/>
          Bengiat a nosus su regnu tuu,
          <br/>
          Siat fatta sa boluntadi tua,
          <br/>
          comenti in celu aici in terra.
          <br/>
          Donasi oi su pani nostu de dogna dii,
          <br/>
          E perdonasi is peccaus nostus,
          <br/>
          Comenti nosus perdonaus a is depidoris nostus.
          <br/>
          E no si lessis arrui in tentatzioni,
          <br/>
          E liberasi de mali.
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          Babbu nostru chi ses in celu,
          <br/>
          Santificau siat su nomine tuu.
          <br/>
          Bengiat a nos su regnu tuu,
          <br/>
          Siat fatta sa boluntade tua,
          <br/>
          comente in celu gasi in terra.
          <br/>
          Dona nos oe su pane nostru de dogna die,
          <br/>
          E perdona nos is peccados nostros,
          <br/>
          Comente nois perdonamus a is depidores nostros.
          <br/>
          E no nos lesses ruer in tentatzione,
          <br/>
          E libera nos de male.
         </p>
        </td>
        <td>
         <p>
          Pater noster qui es in cælis,
          <br/>
          sanctificetur nomen tuum.
          <br/>
          adveniat regnum tuum,
          <br/>
          fiat voluntas tua,
          <br/>
          sicut in cælo et in terra.
          <br/>
          Panem nostrum quotidianum da nobis hodie,
          <br/>
          et dimitte nobis debita nostra,
          <br/>
          sicut et nos dimittimus debitoribus nostris.
          <br/>
          et ne nos inducas in tentationem
          <br/>
          sed libera nos a malo.
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>

          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Sardinian+language+use+survey%2C+1995&amp;rft.pub=Euromosaic&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.uoc.edu%2Feuromosaic%2Fweb%2Fdocument%2Fsard%2Fan%2Fe1%2Fe1.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASardinian+language">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
          To access the data, click on List by languages, Sardinian, then scroll to "Sardinian language use survey".
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-specification-18">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          ^
          <a href="#cite_ref-specification_18-0">
           <sup>
            <i>
             <b>
              a
             </b>
            </i>
           </sup>
          </a>
          <a href="#cite_ref-specification_18-1">
           <sup>
            <i>
             <b>
              b
             </b>
            </i>
           </sup>
          </a>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          It is to be noted on that matter that Wagner conducted academic research in 1951; it took in fact another forty years for Sardinian to be politically recognized, at least formally, as one of Italy's twelve
          <a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">
           minority languages
          </a>
          by the Law no.482/99.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-19">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-19">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Original version (in
          <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
           Italian
          </a>
          ):
          <i>
           Sorge ora la questione se il sardo si deve considerare come un dialetto o come una lingua. È evidente che esso è, politicamente, uno dei tanti dialetti dell’Italia, come lo è anche, p. es., il serbo-croato o l’albanese parlato in vari paesi della Calabria e della Sicilia. Ma dal punto di vista linguistico la questione assume un altro aspetto. Non si può dire che il sardo abbia una stretta parentela con alcun dialetto dell’italiano continentale; è un parlare romanzo arcaico e con proprie spiccate caratteristiche, che si rivelano in un vocabolario molto originale e in una morfologia e sintassi assai differenti da quelle dei dialetti italiani.
          </i>
         </span>
        </li>

          <a class="external text" href="http://www.thelatinlibrary.com/dante/vulgar.shtml" rel="nofollow">
           <i>
            Dantis Alagherii De Vulgari Eloquentia Liber Primus
           </i>
           , The Latin Library
          </a>
          : Sardos etiam, qui non Latii sunt sed Latiis associandi videntur, eiciamus, quoniam soli sine proprio vulgari esse videntur, gramaticam tanquam simie homines imitantes: nam domus nova et dominus meus locuntur. (Lib. I, XI, 7)
         </span>
        </li>

Ego iudice Mariano de Lacon fazo ista carta ad onore de omnes homines de Pisas pro xu toloneu ci mi pecterunt: e ego donolislu pro ca lis so ego amicu caru e itsos a mimi; ci nullu imperatore ci lu aet potestare istu locu de non (n)apat comiatu de leuarelis toloneu in placitu: de non occidere pisanu ingratis: e ccausa ipsoro ci lis aem leuare ingratis, de facerlis iustitia inperatore ci nce aet exere intu locu ...

        <li id="cite_note-64">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-64">
